        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8890acbc5054436eb916ffb186c0f729">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7bb5fbdc-e372-4991-b17f-af3aee98f892'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-6658-0210"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6658-0210</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>College of Materials Science and Engineering, Hunan University, Changsha, Hunan, 410082 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>Hunan Joint International Laboratory of Advanced Materials and Technology for Clean Energy, Hunan University, Changsha, Hunan, 410082 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>Hunan Province Key Laboratory for Advanced Carbon Materials and Applied Technology, Hunan University, Changsha, Hunan, 410082 P. R. China</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:fancl@hnu.edu.cn"><span>fancl@hnu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:liujilei@hnu.edu.cn"><span>liujilei@hnu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Fan/Changling">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Liu/Jilei" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Jilei Liu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d115727689" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Carbon material has emerged as a highly promising anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its abundance of resources, cost-effectiveness, and high carbon yield. This work elaborately designs the precursor structure for the self-assembly of melamine-cyanuric acid on the anthracite surface through hydrogen bonding, and successfully constructs N-doped carbon with tailored microstructure and expanded interlayer spacing. Serving as anode for SIBs, the optimized sample delivers high specific capacity (371.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), superior rate capability (295.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and excellent ultra-long cycling performance (the retention of 91.5% after 3000 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The systematic investigations reveal the enhancement of sodium-ion storage in the low-voltage plateau region involving the interlayer intercalation coupled with nanopores filling. It is discovered that the microporous structure formed by the appropriate graphite sheet angle influences the migration and storage of sodium ions. Density functional theory calculations indicate that the adsorption capacity for sodium ion is enhanced and the migration energy barrier perpendicular to the graphite layer is reduced at the appropriate angle of 8°. This study provides novel insights into the sodium-ion storage mechanism, offering guidance for the better design of anthracite-based carbon anode with superior performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202405174-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>SIBs are attractive options for the development of large-scale energy storage with potential economic benefits due to their abundant resources, low cost, and similar electrochemical properties compared to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0001_R_d115727644e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0003_R_d115727644e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The realization of massive scale-up commercial applications largely relies on the development of high-performance and cost-effective electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0004_R_d115727644e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Rec.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0005_R_d115727644e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the commercial graphite anode for LIBs exhibits low reversible capacity, due to the small interlayer spacing, and large radius of sodium ions (0.102&nbsp;nm), which cannot fulfill the effective intercalation of sodium ions. Additionally, the intercalation compound formed between graphite and sodium is unstable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0006_R_d115727644e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Power Sources">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0007_R_d115727644e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, several anode materials have been applied to SIBs, including carbonaceous materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0008_R_d115727644e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0010_R_d115727644e444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal/alloys,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0011_R_d115727644e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> metal compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0012_R_d115727644e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater.">12</a>-<a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0014_R_d115727644e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and organic compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0015_R_d115727644e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these electrode materials, carbonaceous materials such as hard carbon, have attracted significant attention due to their high electrochemical performance and low cost.</p>
         
         <p>Hard carbon delivers turbostratic structure induced by the <i>sp<sup>3</sup></i>–carbon atom, exhibiting highly disordered structure and expanded interlayer spacing, in which sodium ions can store at the edges, defect sites, pores, and between graphite layers. The precursors of hard carbon material are derived from a wide range of sources, mainly including resin,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0016_R_d115727644e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0017_R_d115727644e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Carbon">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> biomass,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0018_R_d115727644e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Carbon">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0019_R_d115727644e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Energy Chem.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> pitch,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0020_R_d115727644e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0021_R_d115727644e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Energy">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and coal-based materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0022_R_d115727644e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0024_R_d115727644e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although hard carbon derived from resin and biomass exhibits superior capacity, however, the industrial development of these materials faces limitations, due to the high cost of resin and the variability in biomass raw materials. Pitch and coal-based materials characterized by low cost and high yield have attracted attention as promising precursors for commercial carbon materials. However, these carbon materials usually exhibit highly ordered microcrystalline structures with small interlayer spacing, which limits the intercalation of sodium ions. To inhibit the ordered growth of carbon crystallites, Li et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0025_R_d115727644e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Electrochim. Acta">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> introduced oxygen functional groups into the needle coke to expand the interlayer spacing which is beneficial for the intercalation of sodium ions. Xu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0026_R_d115727644e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> utilized the cross-linking interactions of sucrose and lignite to adjust the microstructure of coal-based carbon, leading to the generation of a predominant pseudo-graphitic phase, that delivers high Na-storage capacity and initial coulombic efficiency. Qiu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0027_R_d115727644e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Eng. J.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized highly disordered amorphous carbon by combining the design of the molecular structure with the template method, achieving expanded interlayer distance and good electrical conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to adjusting the microstructure and enlarging the layer spacing for the intercalation of sodium ions, nanopore filling can also enhance the plateau capacity of carbon anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0028_R_d115727644e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0030_R_d115727644e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - SusMat">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0031_R_d115727644e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designed a porous structure of hard carbon material to increase the closed pores in the carbon skeleton, achieving a high plateau capacity of sodium-ion storage. Zhao et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0032_R_d115727644e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> employed the altered thermal conversion pathway to transform open nanopores into closed pores and a high reversible capacity was obtained. Chen et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0033_R_d115727644e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nano-Micro Lett.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> carried out a molten diffusion-carbonization strategy to transform the micropores of carbon into ultra-micropores, providing the extra storage sodium sites.</p>
         
         <p>Understanding the sodium storage mechanism in hard carbon materials is essential to address the associated challenges faced by these materials. Stevens and Dahn<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0034_R_d115727644e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">34</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0035_R_d115727644e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> proposed an early model of the “intercalation/filling” mechanism based on small- and wide-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS, WAXS) analysis to elucidate the sodium storage behavior in hard carbon, assigning the intercalation of sodium ions to the sloping region, and the nanopores filling to the low-voltage plateau region. Cao et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0036_R_d115727644e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> proposed an alternative model “adsorption/intercalation,” and the sloping region was attributed to the surface and defect adsorption, meanwhile the low-voltage plateau region was assigned to the interlayer intercalation, similar to the embedding of lithium between graphite layers. Chen et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0037_R_d115727644e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> found that the plateau capacity of hard carbon was realized through the interlayer intercalation of sodium ions into the micropore filling, and a hybrid sodium storage mechanism of “adsorption-intercalation/filling” was established.</p>
         
         <p>Currently, there is still a great controversy on the sodium storage mechanism in the low-voltage plateau region, primarily hinging on the differences in the microstructure of hard carbon. Most studies have explored the effects of interlayer spacing, heteroatom doping, defects, and microporous structure on the sodium-ion storage capacity. However, the effects of graphite sheet angle and the microporous structure on the migration and storage of sodium ions have not been discussed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0038_R_d115727644e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater.">38</a>-<a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0042_R_d115727644e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nat. Commun.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, it is vital to enhance the low-voltage plateau capacity for achieving superior energy density in SIBs by skillfully manipulating the microstructure and clarifying the impact of microporous structure on sodium storage.</p>
         
         <p>Anthracite, characterized by high carbon content and low cost, has been extensively researched in energy storage applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0043_R_d115727644e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0044_R_d115727644e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Energy Storage Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, coal-based carbon, primarily consisting of polynuclear condensed aromatic hydrocarbons, is prone to stacking carbon layer during the carbonization process, resulting in the highly ordered graphite structure with narrow interlayer spacing and few defects, which is not conducive to the storage of sodium ions. Hence, this work elaborately designed the precursor structure and systematically investigated the sodium storage mechanism of anthracite-based carbon anode to achieve better electrochemical performance. Specifically, we proposed the self-assembly strategy of melamine-cyanuric acid (MCA) molecules on the surface of anthracite through hydrogen bonds to design the structure of the precursor. Utilizing a two-step pyrolysis method, N-doped anthracite-based carbon materials with tailored microstructure and expanded interlayer spacing were designed. Notably, the formation of a carbon nitride (g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>) template played a vital role in the construction of a 3D conductive network and the transformation of microstructure. The influence of N-doping on the microstructure of coal-based carbon was systematically explored. The sodium storage mechanism was revealed by using in situ Raman spectroscopy, ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), and electron paramagnetic resonance technology (EPR). The graphite sheet angle was used to characterize the microporous structure of coal-based carbon. Furthermore, density functional theory calculations (DFT) were carried out to investigate the influence of graphite sheet angle on the storage and migration of sodium ions.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202405174-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202405174-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Analysis and Evolution Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>The hard carbon materials are prepared by the two-step pyrolysis reaction, through the self-assembly strategy of MCA and anthracite as precursors under Ar atmosphere at various temperatures (denoted as NAC-T, T = 1000, 1200, 1400). The preparation is schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0001">1a</a>. The composition of anthracite is shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), showing the characteristics of high carbon content. First, the MCA@anthracite precursor is pretreated at low temperatures to remove the small molecules, strengthen the cross-linking structure, and enhance the interaction of precursor molecules, which is beneficial for the generation of extensive nanopores and disordered carbon. During the thermal polymerization process, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> gradually forms on the surface of coal-based carbon, offering the opportunity for the construction of a 3D conductive network structure and the transformation of microstructure during high-temperature carbonization. Second, in the process of high-temperature carbonization, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> template decomposes to provide a nitrogen source and defect-rich carbon fiber skeleton, which is conducive to the construction of 3D conductive network and flexible skeleton, that enhances the conductivity and structural stability of carbon material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0045_R_d115727644e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Eng. J.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Through adjustment of nitrogen content by high temperatures, the microstructure and interlayer spacing of carbon material are regulated and carbon nanofibers wrapped NAC particles with abundant defects, nanopores, and expanded interlayer spacing are obtained.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405174-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/262f3db0-c062-45bc-adb4-f8a99678b489/adfm202405174-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/262f3db0-c062-45bc-adb4-f8a99678b489/adfm202405174-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/25da8182-5e3d-4474-8d23-1c3273040ba9/adfm202405174-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/262f3db0-c062-45bc-adb4-f8a99678b489/adfm202405174-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405174-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405174" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the formation of NAC. b) XRD patterns, c) Raman spectra, d) XPS survey spectra for AC, NAC-1000, NAC-1200, and NAC-1400. High-resolution N 1s spectra for e) NAC-1000, f) NAC-1200, and g) NAC-1400.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The crystal characteristics of anthracite-based carbon are determined by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman spectra, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0001">1b,c</a>. The XRD patterns show two broad diffraction peaks at ≈24° and 43°, corresponding to the (002) and (100) planes of graphitic carbon material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0046_R_d115727644e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Energy Chem.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with AC, the (002) peak intensities of NAC-1000 and NAC-1200 drop evidently, and the peaks become significantly broader, indicating the increasing disordered structure. This is mainly attributed to the abundant nitrogen doping. When the carbonization temperature increases to 1400&nbsp;°C, the (002) peak of NAC-1400 becomes narrower and slightly shifts to a higher 2<i>θ</i> angle, indicating the enhancement of graphitization during high-temperature carbonization.</p>
            
            <p>The Raman spectra present characteristic peaks of <i>D-</i>band (≈1345&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) and <i>G</i>-band (≈1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>), ascribing to the defect-induced disorder and regular graphitic structure in carbon materials, respectively. The intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub><i>D</i></sub>/<i>I</i><sub><i>G</i></sub>) is widely used to evaluate the graphitization degree of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0047_R_d115727644e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub></i> values for NAC-1000 and NAC-1200 are 1.19 and 1.09, respectively, which are higher than that of AC (1.02), indicating more defects and amorphous structure in N-doped anthracite-based carbon materials obtained at low carbonization temperature. These defects are mainly ascribed to the N-doping and short-range disordered carbon, which is consistent with the previous XRD analysis. With the increase in temperature, the <i>I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub></i> ratio is gradually decreased to 0.96 for NAC-1400, indicating the decrease of defect structure and the increase of graphitic carbon. Therefore, the carbonization temperature can affect the graphitization degree of carbon materials. The XRD and Raman results indicate the doping of nitrogen can introduce defects and regulate the disordered microstructure of carbon materials during the carbonization process.</p>
            
            <p>The elemental composition and chemical valence states of anthracite-based carbon are investigated by using XPS, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0001">1d</a>. Three distinct characteristic peaks corresponding to C 1s (284.7&nbsp;eV), N 1s (399.6&nbsp;eV), and O 1s (531.7&nbsp;eV) are observed in NAC, confirming the successful incorporation of nitrogen in the carbon materials. However, the intensity of N 1s peak gradually decreases with the increasing of carbonization temperature, resulting in the appropriate adjustment of nitrogen content from 5.00 to 1.12&nbsp;at.%, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The high-resolution N 1s spectrum of NAC-1000 can be deconvoluted into four typical peaks at 398.2, 399.5, 401.2, and 403.5&nbsp;eV, corresponding to different nitrogen chemical environments, namely pyridinic N, pyrrolic N, graphitic N, and oxidized N, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0001">1e–g</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0040_R_d115727644e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem. A">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remarkably, the integrated areas of these peaks change significantly with the increase in temperature, indicating the influence of carbonization temperature on the configurations and content of nitrogen. In the case of NAC-1000, the amounts of pyridinic N and pyrrolic N are more than 58% of the total nitrogen content (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). As the carbonization temperature increases gradually, the percentage of pyridinic N decreases significantly and vanishes eventually at 1400&nbsp;°C, while the graphitic N gradually increases. These findings indicate that graphitic N is more stable than pyridinic N and pyrrolic N at high carbonization temperatures. The high-resolution C 1s spectra of NAC are deconvoluted into three peaks as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), where the peak at 284.6&nbsp;eV is attributed to the <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> C─C bond, the peak at 285.4&nbsp;eV is assigned to the <i>sp<sup>3</sup></i> C─O or C─N bond, and the peak at 288.9&nbsp;eV is corresponded to the C═O or C═N bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0028_R_d115727644e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nano Energy">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the increase of carbonization temperature, the intensity of <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> C─C bond enhances gradually, which indicates that the graphitization degree of carbon material is increasing.</p>
            
            <p>The microscopic morphology of anthracite-based carbon is characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM). In <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">2a</a>,<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">e</a>, AC consists of irregular bulk particles with rough surfaces and large sizes. In comparison, NAC exhibits a smooth surface and small particle size, indicating that the doping of nitrogen can inhibit the stacking of carbon layers and the agglomeration of particles during high-temperature carbonization.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405174-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/76f6645e-1c0f-435e-a6ed-15d6b21d962b/adfm202405174-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/76f6645e-1c0f-435e-a6ed-15d6b21d962b/adfm202405174-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f993c647-1e41-4313-b0cd-6a6b23852281/adfm202405174-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/76f6645e-1c0f-435e-a6ed-15d6b21d962b/adfm202405174-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405174-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405174" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM, HRTEM, and SAED images of a,e,i) AC, b,f,j) NAC-1000, c,g,k) NAC-1200, d,h,l) NAC-1400. m) Schematic diagram of the micropores formed by graphite sheet angle. Distribution of graphite sheet angle for n) NAC-1000, o) NAC-1200, p) NAC-1400.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">2b–d,f–h</a> shows that NAC particles are attached by the interwoven carbon nanofibers, with their content positively correlated with the carbonization temperature. At the temperature of 1200&nbsp;°C, the nanofibers in NAC-1200 are well developed, constructing an efficient conductive network and flexible skeleton, which is beneficial to enhance the electrical conductivity and structural stability of carbon material, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). However, it is not conducive to the formation of nanofibers in NAC-1400 at the temperature of 1400&nbsp;°C, damaging the structural stability of carbon material.</p>
            
            <p>The HRTEM image of AC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">2i</a>) reveals an ordered arrangement of carbon crystallites in pristine anthracite-derived carbon with highly graphitized microstructure, displaying regular stacking of carbon layers with an average spacing of 0.362&nbsp;nm. The microchip layers are closely connected and exhibit a densified structure, which is further confirmed by the sharp diffraction rings according to the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern. However, the microstructure of N-doped anthracite-based carbon material is significantly different, exhibiting an amorphous micromorphology, which is further confirmed by the dispersed diffraction rings. This is because nitrogen doping hinders the effective development of the long-range ordered carbon and the accumulation of graphite sheets. Specifically, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">2j</a>, some short and curved carbon layers with random orientations are observed in NAC-1000, indicating the highly disordered structure. Furthermore, NAC-1200 and NAC-1400 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">2k,l</a>) treated at higher temperatures deliver the typical turbostratic graphitic microstructure. The random stacking of curved carbon lattice stripes and the limited rearrangement of carbon layers result in the increase of nanopores, providing sufficient active sites for sodium-ion storage. More importantly, the DigitalMicrograph software is used to evaluate the interplanar spacing of graphite layers in anthracite-based carbon. The average interlayer spacing for NAC-1000, NAC-1200, and NAC-1400 are calculated to be 0.371, 0.394, and 0.373&nbsp;nm, respectively. The expanded interlayer spacing can facilitate the transportation of sodium ions, improving the electrochemical performance of anthracite-based carbon anode. Furthermore, the clarity of diffraction rings in NAC gradually increases from 1000 to 1400&nbsp;°C, indicating the enhancement of local graphitic carbon structure with the increasing temperature, consistent with the Raman analysis.</p>
            
            <p>According to the HRTEM images, it is observed that the turbostratic carbon layers contribute to the formation of nanopore structures. To deeply investigate the correlation between them, we propose a novel method to characterize the angle between graphite sheets, describing the microporous structure of hard carbon. Specifically, we conduct a statistical analysis of the graphite sheet angles from the HRTEM images of NAC by using the ImageJ software. It is defined as the angle between the curved graphite sheets and the parallel carbon layers, as well as the microporous structure formed by them, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">2m</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0002">2n–p</a> presents the normal distribution statistical diagrams of the angles between graphite sheets. The average angles of graphite sheet in hard carbon NAC-1000, NAC-1200, and NAC-1400 are measured to be 12.9, 8.3, and 7.2°, respectively. The results demonstrate that the angle between the graphite sheets decreases with the increase of carbonization temperature, thereby promoting the generation of internal nanopore structure. The arrangement of graphite layers at the appropriate angle of 8° not only contributes to the formation of microporous structures but also expands the interlayer spacing, which accelerates the migration of ions and improves the storage of sodium ions. In contrast, the graphite layers of AC are oriented in horizontal sheets, and the horizontal stacking results in limited interlayer spacing, which hinders the rapid diffusion of ions and the insertion/extraction of ions between graphite layers, thereby compromising the transportation and storage of sodium ions.</p>
            
            <p>The specific surface area and pore size distribution of anthracite-based carbon are characterized by the N<sub>2</sub> absorption/desorption isotherms analysis with the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method. In <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0003">3a</a>, all curves of samples exhibit the shape of type <b>II</b>, indicating the existence of nanopore structures in carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0048_R_d115727644e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - New J. Chem.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption quantity of AC is negligible, exhibiting low specific surface area (0.64&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and pore volume (0.0049&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), which is not favorable for the transportation of sodium ions. In contrast, the specific surface areas of NAC-1000 (5.46&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), NAC-1200 (7.07&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and NAC-1400 (6.84&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) are much higher than that of AC, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). The pore volume also delivers a similar trend. According to the pore size distribution curves obtained by the BJH model in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0003">3b</a>, NAC exhibits the characteristics of mesopores (2–50&nbsp;nm). Specifically, the pore sizes in NAC-1000, NAC-1200, and NAC-1400 are mainly centered at 2.06, 2.58, and 2.57&nbsp;nm, respectively. As a result, the appropriate increase of carbonization temperature optimizes the pore structure, which facilitates the rapid transportation of ions and sufficient penetration of electrolytes.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405174-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92d43384-3466-4d08-959d-efd8228643f4/adfm202405174-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/92d43384-3466-4d08-959d-efd8228643f4/adfm202405174-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c3401222-cf20-4261-9dbb-e0aff89f3bc9/adfm202405174-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92d43384-3466-4d08-959d-efd8228643f4/adfm202405174-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405174-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405174" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms, b) pore size distribution curves, c) SAXS curves for AC, NAC-1000, NAC-1200, and NAC-1400. d) FT-IR spectra of anthracite, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@C and pure g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. e) TG/DSC curves, f) 3D TG-MS curves of MCA@anthracite precursor at different carbonization temperatures under Ar atmosphere. g) Structural evolution mechanism of anthracite-based carbon attached by carbon nanofibers.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">To further analyze the internal pore structure of anthracite-based carbon, SAXS measurement is carried out, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0003">3c</a>. The SAXS patterns display broad humps at the scattering vector <i>q</i> of 0.1–0.4&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>, indicating the existence of internal nanopores. The <i>q</i> is defined as Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202405174-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>).

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405174-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/cf7e1459-0433-4870-91a5-fdab690643e9/adfm202405174-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202405174:adfm202405174-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202405174-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>q</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                              <mi>π</mi>
                              <mi>sin</mi>
                              <mi>θ</mi>
                              <mo>/</mo>
                              <mi>λ</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}q = 4\pi \sin \theta /\lambda \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>θ</i> is the half scattering angle, and <i>λ</i> is the X-ray wavelength (1.5418&nbsp;Å). The scattering in the lower <i>q</i> region (0.02–0.06) is derived from the large-size microstructures, such as meso- or macro-pores. With the increase of carbonization temperature, the humped peak of SAXS pattern becomes more pronounced and shifts to the lower direction of <i>q</i> vector, indicating an increase of nanopore size in carbon materials. The radius of internal nanopores (<i>R</i>) is calculated from the SAXS data of hard carbon material, according to Equations&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202405174-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>) and (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202405174-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0049_R_d115727644e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Exploration">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405174-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a2cb9c9f-b714-4356-a2a0-792347c5f880/adfm202405174-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202405174:adfm202405174-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202405174-math-0002.png">
