                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}I\left( q \right) = A/{{q}^a} + Ba_1^4/{{\left( {1 + a_1^2{{q}^2}} \right)}^2} + D\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405174-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/987e2bb0-30d0-496a-9862-1c63e3bbe1cd/adfm202405174-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202405174:adfm202405174-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202405174-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>R</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msqrt>
                                 <mn>10</mn>
                              </msqrt>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>a</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}R = \sqrt {10} \ {{a}_1}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>I(q)</i> is the scattering intensity, <i>q</i> is the scattering vector, <i>A</i> and <i>B</i> are the proportion of large and small pores to the total surface areas, and <i>D</i> is a constant background term. With the temperature increases from 1000 to 1400&nbsp;°C, the fitted internal nanopore sizes are increased from 1.02 to 2.10&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). It is believed that the high temperature destroys the connection between the adjacent ordered carbon layers, expands the graphitized domains and increases the nanopores.</div>
            
            <p>To well understand the reaction process of precursor, the FT-IR analysis of precursor calcined at a low-temperature stage is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0003">3d</a>. The absorption peak of anthracite at 3000–3500&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the –OH group. Various oxygen-containing functional groups and some aliphatic hydrocarbons are observed at 1000–1700&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. The aromatic hydrocarbons are assigned at 700–900&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0050_R_d115727644e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Environ. Sci. Technol.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the thermal polymerization process of precursor, the absorption peaks of typical aromatic CN heterocycles appear ≈1200–1650&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, and the peak at 809&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is assigned to the s-triazine ring units of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, proving the formation of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> on the surface of carbon derived from anthracite, which is consistent with the XRD analysis (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0051_R_d115727644e1030" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Appl. Phys. A">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the position and intensity of the absorption peaks are different from those of pure g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, resulting from the cross-linking reaction with coal-based carbon.</p>
            
            <p>To gain insights into the formation of anthracite-based carbon material, the precursor MCA@anthracite is characterized by in situ thermogravimetric analysis coupled to mass spectrometry (TG-MS) under Ar atmosphere. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0003">3e</a>, a slight mass loss appears below 300&nbsp;°C, attributed to the release of small molecular gases such as H<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O, and CH<sub>4</sub>. A significant mass loss is observed at 550–750&nbsp;°C. The formation and decomposition of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> template take place in this stage. Two distinct endothermic peaks at 628 and 700&nbsp;°C are observed according to the DSC curve, corresponding to the cross-linking reaction between coal-based carbon with the template and the decomposition of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> template, respectively. Furthermore, it becomes relatively flat at high temperatures.</p>
            
            <p>The evolved gases of precursor MCA@anthracite during the carbonization progress are determined by the TG-MS technology and the 3D profiles are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The gases such as CO, CO<sub>2</sub>, and C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>6</sub> are predominantly released during the cross-linking reaction. At 700&nbsp;°C, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> template decomposes, and a large amount of nitrogen-containing gas species such as N<sub>2</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>N<sub>2</sub> are released, generating defect-rich carbon, and preventing the accumulation of graphite sheets.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0039_R_d115727644e1087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Small">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The mass loss is gentle in the range of 800–1000&nbsp;°C and represents the transformation process of N-doped amorphous carbon, which effectively inhibits the carbonization of carbon material at high temperatures. With the further increase of carbonization temperature, the microstructure of hard carbon material is well-developed and the interlayer spacing is efficiently adjusted, which is beneficial for the storage of sodium ions.</p>
            
            <p>Based on the above analysis, we propose a possible structural formation mechanism for anthracite-based carbon attached by carbon nanofibers without metal catalysis, schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0003">3g</a>. The structure of the precursor is designed by the self-assembly of MCA molecules on the surface of anthracite through hydrogen bonding. At a low-temperature stage below 600&nbsp;°C, the layered g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> structure is gradually formed on the surface of coal-based carbon from melamine-cyanuric acid molecules, accompanied by the release of small gas molecules such as H<sub>2</sub>, H<sub>2</sub>O, and CH<sub>4</sub>, which enhances the cross-linking between g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and anthracite and inhibits the accumulation of carbon layers. When the temperature rises over 750&nbsp;°C, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> template is completely decomposed, accompanied by the release of some organic gas molecules as well as nitrogen- and oxygen-containing gases. During the thermal polymerization process, abundant C─N• or C─C• radicals are generated on the surface of coal-based carbon, constructing effective active centers for the generation of carbon nanofibers. At the same time, abundant defective carbon is generated inside the anthracite-based carbon. Under the driving of high temperatures, the gas molecules adsorbed on the catalytic center undergo a cracking reaction and generate active carbon atoms, which are continually deposited on the surface of coal-based carbon. The carbon–carbon interactions are taken place and the <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i>-bonded graphene sheets are formed, constituting the core for the effective growth of nanofibers. With the emergence of the carbon core, some other graphene sheets and carbon nanoparticles migrate, deposit, and grow on the surface, ultimately resulting in the formation of carbon nanofiber structure. Additionally, the doping of nitrogen promotes the deformation of carbon lattice, facilitating the bend and growth of carbon nanofiber, and enhancing the interconnections between carbon surfaces. Finally, carbon nanofibers are effectively generated on the surface of anthracite-based carbon.</p>
            
            <p>Meanwhile, the defect-rich carbon formed inside the anthracite-based carbon can effectively prevent the growth of long-range ordered carbon and the accumulation of graphite sheets. As the temperature further increases, the doping content of nitrogen and configuration in carbon materials are adjusted, and the microstructure of hard carbon material is well-developed, including the interlayer spacing, micropore, and graphite sheet angle.</p>
            
            <p>The appropriate nitrogen doping adjusts the angle of the graphite sheet inside coal-based carbon, promotes the formation of abundant micropores, and expands the interlayer spacing, thereby accelerating the transportation of ions and electrons, and providing sufficient space for the storage of sodium ions. However, the doping content of nitrogen decreases and the local graphitized carbon develops orderly when the carbonization temperature is further increased. As the graphitization degree increases, the angle of the graphite sheet and interlayer spacing decrease, which is not conducive to the transportation and storage of sodium ions. Therefore, by regulating the doping content of nitrogen at appropriate temperatures, the microporous structure formed by tailoring the graphite sheet angle and expanding the interlayer spacing can promote the migration and storage of sodium ions in anthracite-based carbon anode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202405174-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance and Kinetics Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The sodium-ion storage performance of anthracite-based carbon anode is evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic discharge/charge tests in half-coin cells. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4a</a> presents the CV curves in the first cycle for the NAC anode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 0.0–2.5&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). In the first cathodic scan, a broad irreversible cathodic peak <b>I</b> centered at 0.35–0.54&nbsp;V is observed for all samples, which is attributed to the decomposition of electrolyte on the electrode surface, resulting in the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0052_R_d115727644e1148" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Small">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, AC and NAC-1000 exhibit large irreversible peaks, indicating that severe irreversible reactions occurred on the electrode surface, affecting the electrochemical performance of the electrode. Additionally, a pair of sharp redox peaks <b>II/III</b> appear in the range of 0.0–0.15&nbsp;V, corresponding to the insertion/extraction of sodium ions between the graphitic carbon layers or nanopores. However, the redox peak of AC is obviously shifted, implying large electrochemical polarization and high charge transfer resistance, attributed to the small carbon interlayer spacing in the densified microstructure of coal-based carbon. The CV curves in the subsequent cycles are nearly overlapped (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), indicating highly reversible insertion/extraction of sodium ions in the carbon anode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405174-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1e5d0c9e-d5a1-4e1a-8348-7b63cc7228e4/adfm202405174-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1e5d0c9e-d5a1-4e1a-8348-7b63cc7228e4/adfm202405174-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05492f8d-b441-483b-90e8-573238669462/adfm202405174-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1e5d0c9e-d5a1-4e1a-8348-7b63cc7228e4/adfm202405174-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405174-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405174" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves in the first cycle, b) galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles in the initial cycle at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, c) rate performance for AC, NAC-1000, NAC-1200, and NAC-1400. d) Comparison of rate performance for NAC-1200 in this work and carbon anode reported in some literature. e) Long-term cycling performance at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, f) the contribution ratio of capacitive capacity at different scan rates, g) Nyquist plots, and h) <i>Z'</i> ∼ <i>ω<sup>−1/2</sup></i> plots for AC, NAC-1000, NAC-1200,&nbsp;and NAC-1400.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4b</a> displays the galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of anthracite-based carbon anode at the current density of 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The initial reversible capacity of NAC-1200 is 371.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of NAC-1000 (223.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), NAC-1400 (288.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and AC (260.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). NAC-1000 exhibits low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 64.7%, resulting in significant irreversible capacity loss of 121.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This indicates severe irreversible reactions are taking place between the electrode surface and electrolytes, leading to the formation of thick SEI film. As the carbonization temperature increases, the ICE values of NAC-1200 (74.2%) and NAC-1400 (77.5%) gradually increase, which is attributed to the reduction in defective carbon during high-temperature carbonization, thereby reducing the formation of SEI film.</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, the discharge/charge curves of all samples exhibit two distinct regions, which correlates well with the CV analysis. The low-voltage plateau region below 0.15&nbsp;V is attributed to the insertion/extraction of sodium ions between carbon layers or nanopores. The high-voltage sloping region above 0.15&nbsp;V corresponds to the adsorption/desorption of sodium ions on the surface, edges, and defects of carbon material. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the capacity contributions of the plateau/sloping regions are 92.7/130.4 for NAC-1000, 179.3/192.0 for NAC-1200, and 165.3/123.6 for NAC-1400 in the first cycle, respectively. Namely, the corresponding proportion of plateau capacity (i.e., 41.5%, 48.3%, 57.2%) increases gradually with the increasing temperature, indicating that the carbonization temperature can control the N-doping and adjust the microstructure of anthracite-based carbon material. Furthermore, NAC-1200 anode exhibits the optimum plateau capacity and sloping capacity, which is attributed to the tailored microstructure, controllable defects, and expanded interlayer spacing.</p>
            
            <p>To gain further insights into the electrochemical performance, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4c</a> presents the rate capacity of the NAC anode. Notably, the rate performance of NAC-1200 anode differs significantly from other samples, exhibiting the most favorable performance. Specifically, NAC-1200 delivers reversible capacities of 371.3, 369.6, 356.6, 341.5, 329.3, 318.5, and 305.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Remarkably, even at the high current density of 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (i.e., 33&nbsp;C, 1&nbsp;C = 300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), the capacity remains as high as 295.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, outperforming the carbon-based anode reported in the literature (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0044_R_d115727644e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Energy Storage Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0053_R_d115727644e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Carbon">53</a>-<a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0057_R_d115727644e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Energy Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), NAC-1200 exhibits the smallest electrochemical polarization with the increasing current density, attributed to the well-established 3D conductive network, appropriate expanded interlayer spacing, as well as suitable graphite sheet angle. However, the electrochemical polarization of AC is very large, ascribing to the few defects and small graphite interlayer spacing. Furthermore, the long-term cycling performance of NAC is evaluated for 3000 cycles at the current density of 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4e</a>. Obviously, NAC-1200 exhibits the best long-term cycling performance. Even after 3000 discharge/charge cycles, NAC-1200 delivers outstanding capacity retention of 91.5% with a remaining reversible capacity of 313.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent coulombic efficiency close to 100%. In contrast, the capacity of AC undergoes significant deterioration from 140.7 to 48.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in the poor capacity retention of 34.6% after 3000 cycles, attributed to the dense stacking with limited interlayer spacing and the unstable structure.</p>
            
            <p>It is believed that the superior rate performance and excellent cycling stability of NAC-1200 are attributed to the synergistic effect of morphology and microstructure. On the one hand, the irregular block particles with small sizes are attached to the 3D network structure and flexible framework constructed by the interwoven carbon nanofibers. Therefore, it enhances the electrical conductivity and structural stability of anthracite-based carbon material, accelerates the transportation of ions, and buffers the volume expansion caused by the insertion/extraction of sodium ions in the discharge/charge process. On the other hand, the collaborative effect of the appropriate doping of nitrogen and nanopore structure formed by the angle of curved graphite sheets can enhance the surface and the interlayer reactivity of anthracite-based carbon material, significantly contributing to excellent sodium-ion storage. Meanwhile, NAC-1200 exhibits the optimum microstructure with an appropriate angle of turbostratic graphite sheet, along with the expanded interlayer spacing, which facilitates the rapid transportation of ions into carbon layers or nanopores.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To gain a comprehensive understanding of the reaction kinetics of NAC-1200 anode, CV measurements are further conducted at different scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). The functional relationship between the peak current (<i>i)</i> and scan rate (<i>v</i>) is described by Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202405174-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a>), enabling the determination of kinetic information for anthracite-based carbon anode. Where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants, and <i>b</i> can be calculated from the slope of the linear plot ln(<i>i</i>) ∼ ln(<i>v</i>).

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405174-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/80ba7244-0cc2-4e89-a386-d5763bef83c3/adfm202405174-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202405174:adfm202405174-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202405174-math-0004.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mi>b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{{v}^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Additionally, <i>b</i> = 0.5 represents the diffusion-controlled process, and <i>b</i> = 1.0 represents the surface-induced capacitive behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0058_R_d115727644e1302" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Mater.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>b</i> values of cathodic and anodic peaks of NAC-1200 are calculated to be 0.86 and 0.79, indicating that the surface-induced capacitive behavior is dominant. However, the <i>b</i> value of AC is 0.54 and 0.38 (close to 0.5) for the cathodic and anodic peaks, suggesting the predominance of diffusion-controlled process. It is concluded that the kinetics process of AC is quite different from that of NAC, attributed to the doping of nitrogen. Furthermore, the contribution proportion of these two electrochemical processes can be quantified by Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202405174-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>). Where <i>k<sub>1</sub>v</i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> correspond to the contribution of capacitive behavior and diffusion-controlled process, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0059_R_d115727644e1326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Chem. Eng. J.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405174-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f45afa6e-7a6b-4f6e-839c-d2f96ef1a074/adfm202405174-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202405174:adfm202405174-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202405174-math-0005.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = {{k}_1}v + {{k}_2}{{v}^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>At the scan rate of 0.4&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the capacitive contribution of NAC-1200 is 68.2%. The contribution of capacitive behavior further rises with the increasing scan rate, reaching 88.2% at the scan rate of 2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the capacitive behavior endows NAC-1200 with remarkable reversible capacity and superior rate capability. In contrast, AC presents a relatively low capacitive contribution ratio of 21.6% at the scan rate of 0.4&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> and 40.4% at 2.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, attributed to the dense and highly ordered microstructure. Overall, these findings highlight the favorable impact of surface-induced capacitive contribution on the enhanced rate performance of anthracite-based carbon anode, particularly at high current density.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is employed to further investigate the difference in electrochemical performance for NAC anode, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4g</a>. The semicircle in the high-frequency region corresponds to the charge transfer resistance (<i>R<sub>ct</sub></i>), while the sloping line in the low-frequency region represents the Warburg impedance (<i>Z<sub>w</sub></i>) associated with the ion diffusion process. Based on the equivalent circuit model, the fitting results are obtained and summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> of NAC-1200 (2.0&nbsp;Ω) is much lower than that of AC (18.7&nbsp;Ω), which facilitates the efficient charge transfer and enhances the kinetic characteristics. In addition, the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions is calculated by Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202405174-disp-0006" title="Link to equation">6</a>). Where <i>R</i> denotes the ideal gas constant, <i>T</i> refers to the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode, <i>F</i> corresponds to the Faraday constant, <i>n</i> is the transfer number of electrons per molecule during the reaction, <i>C</i> denotes the molar concentration of sodium ions, and <i>σ</i> represents the slope of <i>Z'</i> ∼ <i>ω<sup>−1/2</sup></i> plot.

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202405174-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/32a6d590-60f6-449a-bc26-bd45fb4cc878/adfm202405174-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202405174:adfm202405174-math-0006" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202405174-math-0006.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>R</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>T</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <mo>/</mo>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>A</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>n</mi>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>F</mi>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>C</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>σ</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}D = {{R}^2}{{T}^2}/2{{A}^2}{{n}^4}{{F}^4}{{C}^2}{{\sigma }^2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>According to the fitting plot of <i>Z'</i> and <i>ω<sup>−1/2</sup></i> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0004">4h</a>, the calculated diffusion coefficient of NAC-1200 is 4.5&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of AC (1.3&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−13</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), NAC-1000 (1.2&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), and NAC-1400 (2.1&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−12</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), as listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). This significant improvement of diffusion coefficient for NAC is attributed to the optimized microstructure with appropriate doping of nitrogen, appropriate graphite sheet angle, and enlarged interlayer spacing, which are beneficial to increase the migration of sodium ions. Therefore, anthracite-based carbon anode NAC-1200 exhibits outstanding electrochemical performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202405174-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodium-Ion Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>To unravel the sodium-ion storage mechanism of anthracite-based carbon anode, the in situ Raman, ex situ XRD, and XPS are conducted. In <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0005">5a</a>, it is apparent that the changes of the intensity, position, and shape of peaks <i>D</i> and <i>G</i> for NAC-1200 are minimal prior to be discharged to 0.3&nbsp;V. This indicates that the adsorption of sodium ions on the surface defective sites delivers negligible impact on the breathing vibration of <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> atoms in the carbon rings. Subsequently, the intensity of peak <i>G</i> decreases when the voltage drops to 0.1&nbsp;V, accompanied by a noticeable redshift. This phenomenon is attributed to the disruption of the graphene layer structure after the intercalation of sodium ions, indicating the formation of graphite intercalation compounds (GICs). A pronounced downshift of peak <i>G</i> is observed and the peak broadens into an asymmetrical Fanoy resonance shape when discharges to 0.001&nbsp;V, attributed to the generation of low-stage GICs. Meanwhile, the <i>I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub></i> ratios increase significantly during the intercalation phase, signifying the increased randomness of the carbonaceous structure caused by the expansion of carbon layers after the intercalation of sodium ions.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405174-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b83f15cf-2b74-4ace-a877-1af72a84bd95/adfm202405174-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b83f15cf-2b74-4ace-a877-1af72a84bd95/adfm202405174-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7855a126-a513-4cfb-a589-a05d09f90b99/adfm202405174-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b83f15cf-2b74-4ace-a877-1af72a84bd95/adfm202405174-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405174-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405174" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ Raman spectra and the contour plot of a) NAC-1200 and b) AC. c) Ex situ Na 1s XPS spectra, d) EPR spectra of NAC-1200 at the pristine condition and discharges to 0.2, 0.1, and 0.001&nbsp;V state. e) Schematic illustration of sodium-ion storage behavior during the discharge process.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Notably, the existence of GICs does not preclude the possibility of the filling of nanopores in the low-voltage region. When the voltage is reduced to 0.001&nbsp;V, the attenuation and broadening of peak <i>D</i> may also be related to the filling of sodium ions into nanopores due to the constraint on the <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> ring vibrations. These structural changes gradually recover with the extraction of sodium ions during the charging process, indicating the excellent reversibility of the NAC-1200 anode. Additionally, the ex situ XRD analysis further reveals a slight shift of (002) peak toward the lower angle and the expandable layer space upon discharge to 0.001&nbsp;V, confirming the increased intercalation of sodium ions into the carbon layers and the formation of low-stage GICs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>For AC anode as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0005">5b</a>, there are no significant changes in the intensity, position, or shape of peaks <i>D</i> and <i>G</i> throughout the entire cycle, and the corresponding <i>I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub></i> ratios remain stable, ruling out the intercalation of sodium ions in AC anode and the formation of GICs. Moreover, in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a> (Supporting Information), the absence of noticeable shift in the (002) peak further indicates that the sodium intercalation behavior is negligible. Therefore, the sodium-ion storage mechanism of pristine AC anode is quite different from that of NAC anode in the discharge/charge process, which can be attributed to the variation in their microstructure induced by nitrogen doping.</p>
            
            <p>The above result is further verified by ex situ XPS analysis. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0005">5c</a> presents the XPS spectra of Na 1s for NAC-1200 anode at the different discharge states. The Na 1s peak on the pristine electrode is weak and broad, attributed to the sodium alginate binder. When it discharges to 0.2&nbsp;V, the intensity of Na 1s peak increases obviously, while the peak position does not change significantly. As the discharge drops to 0.1&nbsp;V, the peak position gradually shifts. When it discharges further to 0.001&nbsp;V, the Na 1s peak shifts toward higher binding energy located between sodium metal and sodium ion, implying the presence of metallic Na in the anode. It is concluded that the quasi-metallic sodium is formed on the surface of NAC-1200 anode in the discharge process.</p>
            
            <p>The generation of quasi-metallic sodium is further proved by EPR spectroscopy, which is a powerful technique for discerning the unpaired electrons of alkali metals. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0005">5d</a>, the pristine NAC-1200 anode does not exhibit a pronounced EPR signal, indicating the absence of free radicals and delocalized electrons on the <i>π</i>-conjugated carbon atom. As the discharge process continues to 0.2&nbsp;V, the EPR signal appears evidently. When the discharge voltage reaches 0.1&nbsp;V, the EPR displays an obvious narrow peak centered at the <i>g</i> value of 2.002, proving the presence of free radicals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202405174-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202405174-bib-0060_R_d115727644e1597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Energy Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon further discharging to 0.001&nbsp;V, the peak position of the free radical does not change notably, while the intensity enhances significantly, demonstrating the presence of abundant radicals in NAC-1200 anode. Therefore, the EPR analysis further proves the formation of metallic or quasi-metallic sodium in the discharge process at low voltage. Based on the above analysis, in addition to the adsorption in the sloping region, it is concluded that the “intercalation/filling” hybrid behavior occurs in the low-voltage region, as schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0005">5e</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202405174-sec-0060-title">2.4 DFT Calculations</h3>
            
            <p>The schematic diagram (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0006">6a</a>) illustrates the migration and storage of sodium ions in the microporous structure formed by the graphite sheet angle. Through analysis of the effects of different angles of graphite sheet on the sodium storage capacity and rate performance of anthracite-based carbon anode in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0006">6b,c</a>, reveals that the storage performance of sodium ions initially increases and then decreases as the sheet angle increases. The optimal storage performance is found to occur at the sheet angle of 8°.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202405174-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/252b22a3-bb68-4c60-b733-d88e667442ec/adfm202405174-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/252b22a3-bb68-4c60-b733-d88e667442ec/adfm202405174-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c8e76dbe-c9ca-40e6-b3b5-5099597e60ad/adfm202405174-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/252b22a3-bb68-4c60-b733-d88e667442ec/adfm202405174-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202405174-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202405174" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the migration and storage of sodium ions in the micropore. b,c) The storage capacity and rate performance of sodium ions versus the graphite sheet angle. d) Schematic illustration of graphite fragment with various angles. e) Relative adsorption energies of sodium ions versus the graphite sheet angle. f,g) The migration energy barriers and paths of sodium ions in the gaps of the graphite layer based on the sheet angle of 8°. h) Differential charge density plots of graphite sheet and sodium ions at different entrapment angles and the corresponding Bader charges of sodium ions. The isosurface value is 0.001&nbsp;e&nbsp;bohr<sup>−3</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To gain deeper insights, we subsequently conduct DFT calculations to clarify the effect of the angle between the graphite sheets on the storage performance of sodium ions. First and foremost, the graphite sheets with different angles of 0, 1, 2, 5, 8, and 10° are created by the ASE program, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0006">6d</a>). To match the definite structure of anthracite-based carbon material, we fixed the graphite layer spacing to 3.7&nbsp;Å, which is larger than the conventional graphite layer spacing (3.35&nbsp;Å). Besides, the edges of the graphite fragments are covered with saturated hydrogen to avoid the direct bonding of graphite fragments during the structure optimization. We calculate the adsorption property of sodium ions by graphite with different angles, and the adsorption capability of sodium ions appears to increase and then decrease with the increase of sheet angle with respect to the perfectly parallel structure (0°). It is discovered that the best adsorption energy is −0.62&nbsp;eV at the angle of 8° (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0006">6e</a>), providing an explanation for the superior performance observed at this angle.</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, we have calculated the energy barriers for the migration path of sodium ions in the gaps of the graphite layer based on the sheet angle of 8°, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0006">6f,g</a>. Sodium ions migrating perpendicular to the graphite layer (<b>Path I</b>) following a zigzag path requires a mobility barrier as low as 0.4&nbsp;eV. However, the mobility barrier moving along the gaps in the graphite layer (<b>Path II</b>) is as high as 1.7&nbsp;eV. Through the differential charge density analysis as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202405174-fig-0006">6h</a>, the range of the corresponding charge isopotential surface increases gradually with the increase of the angle between graphite sheets. The cyan region of sodium ions and the yellow region of graphite sheets become large, indicating that more electrons flow from the graphite sheet to the sodium ions. This also explains the strong adsorption effect of large-angle graphite sheets on the sodium ions. In addition, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the Bader charge of sodium ions in NAC-1200 at the graphite sheet angle of 8° is at the lowest value of 0.788 e, which is consistent with the differential charge data. In summary, we investigate the microstructure of anthracite-based carbon material from the viewpoint of graphite sheet angle. It is concluded that the microporous graphite structure formed by the appropriate sheet angle is favorable for the adsorption and migration of sodium ions.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202405174-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, N-doped hard carbon with well-controlled microstructure, defects, and expanded interlayer spacing is successfully synthesized by the self-assembly strategy of melamine-cyanuric acid and anthracite. The generated g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> not only provides an N-rich source to effectively inhibit the growth of long-range ordered crystallites but also constructs a conductive network and flexible framework for enhancing the conductivity and structural stability of anthracite-based carbon material. The optimized NAC-1200 exhibits a high specific capacity of 371.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and ultrahigh rate capacity of 295.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (i.e., 33&nbsp;C), along with extended cycle life exceeding 3000 cycles. Through the analysis of in situ Raman, ex situ XPS, EPR, and DFT calculations, the effect of microstructure on the sodium-ion storage mechanism is systematically elucidated. The findings reveal that the microporous structure formed by the appropriate graphite sheet angle significantly enhances the adsorption and migration of sodium ions, providing novel insights into sodium storage mechanisms. This study contributes innovative ideas for sodium-ion storage, promoting the further development of anthracite-based carbon material.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202405174-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202405174-sec-0110-title"> Synthesis of Precursor</h4>
                  
                  <p>In a typical reaction, 1.00&nbsp;g of melamine (AR, Aladdin) and 1.02&nbsp;g of cyanuric acid (AR, Aladdin) were separately dissolved into deionized water under heating. Subsequently, 1.00&nbsp;g of anthracite (Yunnan province, China) and 0.02&nbsp;g of sodium dodecyl sulfate surfactant (AR, Aladdin) were both introduced into the melamine solution followed by continuous stirring for 1&nbsp;h to obtain uniformly dispersed suspension. Then, the cyanuric acid solution was added dropwise into the suspension and stirred vigorously for 3&nbsp;h to facilitate the self-assembly of melamine-cyanuric acid molecules on the anthracite surface through hydrogen bonds. After centrifugation, washing, and drying at 80&nbsp;°C, the precursor of MCA@anthracite was obtained.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202405174-sec-0120-title"> Synthesis of NAC</h4>
                  
                  <p>The as-obtained precursor was transferred to the vacuum tube furnace and preheated to 600&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h at a heating rate of 2&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> under Ar atmosphere. Subsequently, it was further sintered to different temperatures (1000, 1200, 1400&nbsp;°C) at a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> for high-temperature carbonization. The prepared samples were labeled as NAC-1000, NAC-1200, and NAC-1400. For comparison, a control sample denoted as AC was synthesized by the direct carbonization of anthracite at 600&nbsp;°C and 1200&nbsp;°C without the addition of melamine and cyanuric acid.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202405174-sec-0130-title"> Material Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The structures of as-prepared samples were characterized by an XRD diffractometer (Rigaku Smartlab, Japan) equipped with Cu Ka radiation (<i>λ&nbsp;</i>=&nbsp;1.5418&nbsp;Å) and Raman spectrometer (Renishaw inVia, United Kingdom) at room temperature. XPS spectra were obtained by using an X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (ESCALAB 250 xi, Thermo Fisher Scientific, United States of America). The FT-IR spectra were conducted by using an infrared spectrometer (Nicolet iS10, Thermo Fisher Scientific). The BET-specific surface area and pore size distribution were determined by using an adsorption/desorption analyzer (Micrometrics ASAP 2020, Micromeritics, United States of America) at 77&nbsp;K. The TG analysis was conducted on a synchronous thermal analyzer (TGA/DSC 3+, Mettler, Switzerland) at a heating rate of 10&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup> under Ar atmosphere. The evolved gases during the sintering process were determined by using a synchro-thermogravimetric analyzer (STA 449 F5 Jupiter, Netzsch, Germany) coupled with a mass spectrometer (QMS 403 D, Netzsch, Germany). The morphologies were determined by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Tescan Mira3, Czech Republic). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) and SAED patterns were obtained by transmission electron microscope (FEI Tecnai G2 F30, United States of America). The EPR spectra were recorded on the Bruker EMXplus spectrometer system.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202405174-sec-0140-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The anodes for SIBs were prepared by mixing the active material NAC, carbon black as the conductive agent, and sodium alginate as the binder in a mass ratio of 8:1:1. This mixture was dispersed in deionized water and vigorously stirred for 6&nbsp;h to obtain a homogeneous slurry. The slurry was then coated onto a copper foil collector and subsequently dried in a vacuum oven at 80&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The loading mass of the active material on the working electrode was 1.5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. CR2032-type coin cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glove box. The cells were consisted of the prepared anode as the working electrode, metallic sodium sheet as the counter electrode, glass fiber (Whatman GF/D, United Kingdom) as the separator, and 1.0&nbsp;mol&nbsp;L<sup>−1</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in dimethyl ether (DME) as the electrolyte. The assembled batteries were subjected to galvanostatic discharge/charge tests by using the Land battery test system (CT-2001A, Wuhan, China) within the voltage range of 0.0–2.5&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). CV tests were performed by using a CHI660E electrochemical workstation. EIS measurements were also conducted on the electrochemical workstation within the frequency range of 10<sup>−2</sup>–10<sup>5</sup>&nbsp;Hz with an amplitude of 5.0&nbsp;mV.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202405174-sec-0150-title"> In Situ Raman measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>In situ Raman analysis was carried out by using a customized electrolytic cell device with a quartz plate serving as an optical window for the test. Raman spectra were recorded on a confocal Raman spectrometer (WITEC alpha-300R, Germany) equipped with a laser excitation wavelength of 532&nbsp;nm.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202405174-sec-0160-title"> Ex Situ XRD measurement</h4>
                  
                  <p>The coin cells were disassembled within an Ar-filled glove box (H<sub>2</sub>O / O<sub>2</sub> ≤ 0.01&nbsp;ppm) after reaching the desired voltage. Subsequently, the electrodes were gently washed with the solvent of dimethyl carbonate (DMC). After washing, the electrodes were dried and then sealed in an aluminum plastic bag for further testing. Ex situ analysis was conducted on an XRD diffractometer (Rigaku Smartlab, Japan).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202405174-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202405174-sec-0170-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>To investigate the sodium-ion storage mechanism of anthracite-based carbon with the graphite sheet angle, the DFT calculations were applied within the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) code regarding both structure optimization and reaction pathways. The projector augmented wave (PAW) method and the standard Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) form of the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) were used to describe the interaction between the valence electrons and ionic cores and the exchange-correlation functional, respectively.</p>
                  
                  <p>All models were constructed based on the Atomic Simulation Environment (ASE) and visual software VESTA. A parameter (ISIF = 4) was set to guarantee no change in graphite volume. Besides, the graphite layer spacing is kept at 3.7&nbsp;Å in agreement with hard carbon experimental data. The length of the cell <i>b</i> axis is set to <i>b</i>*(cos<i>θ</i>+1)/2 to ensure that the graphite sheet angle will not change drastically during the structural optimization due to steric hindrance factors, where <i>θ</i> is the setting angle. The study has set the cutoff energy of the plane-wave basis to 450&nbsp;eV based on the ultrafine quality. The Brillouin zone was sampled with a 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;1&nbsp;×&nbsp;8&nbsp;k-point mesh. A residual force threshold of 0.01&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup> was used for geometry optimization. During the geometry optimization, the Van de Waals correction was conducted through the DFT-D3 method. The migration paths of sodium ions were calculated using the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method. Due to the graphite sheet orientation, the migration along the graphite layer direction and the vertical direction were selected in this work.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
